<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Board game</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Genre of seated tabletop social play</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_110713-N-NT881-124_Personnel_Specialist_2nd_Class_James_Vail,_left,_and_Boatswain%27s_Mate_2nd_Class_Nathaniel_Eaton_play_board_games_with_ch.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3888" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/US_Navy_110713-N-NT881-124_Personnel_Specialist_2nd_Class_James_Vail%2C_left%2C_and_Boatswain%27s_Mate_2nd_Class_Nathaniel_Eaton_play_board_games_with_ch.jpg/330px-US_Navy_110713-N-NT881-124_Personnel_Specialist_2nd_Class_James_Vail%2C_left%2C_and_Boatswain%27s_Mate_2nd_Class_Nathaniel_Eaton_play_board_games_with_ch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/US_Navy_110713-N-NT881-124_Personnel_Specialist_2nd_Class_James_Vail%2C_left%2C_and_Boatswain%27s_Mate_2nd_Class_Nathaniel_Eaton_play_board_games_with_ch.jpg/495px-US_Navy_110713-N-NT881-124_Personnel_Specialist_2nd_Class_James_Vail%2C_left%2C_and_Boatswain%27s_Mate_2nd_Class_Nathaniel_Eaton_play_board_games_with_ch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/US_Navy_110713-N-NT881-124_Personnel_Specialist_2nd_Class_James_Vail%2C_left%2C_and_Boatswain%27s_Mate_2nd_Class_Nathaniel_Eaton_play_board_games_with_ch.jpg/660px-US_Navy_110713-N-NT881-124_Personnel_Specialist_2nd_Class_James_Vail%2C_left%2C_and_Boatswain%27s_Mate_2nd_Class_Nathaniel_Eaton_play_board_games_with_ch.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_110713-N-NT881-124_Personnel_Specialist_2nd_Class_James_Vail,_left,_and_Boatswain%27s_Mate_2nd_Class_Nathaniel_Eaton_play_board_games_with_ch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The board game <i><a href="/wiki/Monopoly_(game)" title="Monopoly (game)">Monopoly</a></i> is licensed in 103 countries and printed in 37 languages.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pelip%C3%A4iv%C3%A4_Iisalmen_kaupunginkirjastossa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="683" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Pelip%C3%A4iv%C3%A4_Iisalmen_kaupunginkirjastossa.jpg/330px-Pelip%C3%A4iv%C3%A4_Iisalmen_kaupunginkirjastossa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Pelip%C3%A4iv%C3%A4_Iisalmen_kaupunginkirjastossa.jpg/495px-Pelip%C3%A4iv%C3%A4_Iisalmen_kaupunginkirjastossa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Pelip%C3%A4iv%C3%A4_Iisalmen_kaupunginkirjastossa.jpg/660px-Pelip%C3%A4iv%C3%A4_Iisalmen_kaupunginkirjastossa.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pelip%C3%A4iv%C3%A4_Iisalmen_kaupunginkirjastossa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Young girls playing a board game in the <a href="/wiki/Iisalmi" title="Iisalmi">Iisalmi</a>, Finland, library in 2016</div></div></div>
<p><b>Board games</b> are <a href="/wiki/Tabletop_game" title="Tabletop game">tabletop games</a> that typically use <dfn id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_board_games#pieces" title="Glossary of board games"><span class="glossary-link" style="color:inherit;" title="See entry at: Glossary of board games § pieces">pieces</span></a></dfn>. These pieces are moved or placed on a pre-marked board (playing surface) and often include elements of <a href="/wiki/Tabletop_game" title="Tabletop game">table</a>, <a href="/wiki/Card_game" title="Card game">card</a>, <a href="/wiki/Role-playing_game" title="Role-playing game">role-playing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Miniatures_game" title="Miniatures game">miniatures games</a> as well.
</p><p>Many board games feature a competition between two or more players. To show a few examples: in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Draughts" title="Draughts">checkers</a> (British English name 'draughts'), a player wins by capturing all opposing pieces, while <a href="/wiki/Eurogame" title="Eurogame">Eurogames</a> often end with a calculation of final scores. <i><a href="/wiki/Pandemic_(board_game)" title="Pandemic (board game)">Pandemic</a></i> is a <a href="/wiki/Cooperative_board_game" title="Cooperative board game">cooperative game</a> where players all win or lose as a team, and <a href="/wiki/Peg_solitaire" title="Peg solitaire">peg solitaire</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Puzzle" title="Puzzle">puzzle</a> for one person.
</p><p>There are many varieties of board games. Their representation of real-life situations can range from having no inherent theme, such as <a href="/wiki/Checkers" title="Checkers">checkers</a>, to having a specific theme and narrative, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Cluedo" title="Cluedo">Cluedo</a></i>. Rules can range from the very simple, such as in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Snakes_and_Ladders" title="Snakes and Ladders">Snakes and Ladders</a>; to deeply complex, as in <i><a href="/wiki/Advanced_Squad_Leader" title="Advanced Squad Leader">Advanced Squad Leader</a></i>. Play components now often include custom figures or shaped counters, and distinctively shaped player pieces commonly known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meeples" title="Meeples">meeples</a> as well as traditional cards and dice.
</p><p>The time required to learn or master game play varies greatly from game to game, but is not necessarily related to the number or complexity of rules, and games like <a href="/wiki/Chess" title="Chess">chess</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Go_(board_game)" title="Go (board game)">Go</a> possess relatively simple <dfn id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_board_games#ruleset" title="Glossary of board games"><span class="glossary-link" style="color:inherit;" title="See entry at: Glossary of board games § ruleset">rulesets</span></a></dfn>, but have great strategic depth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Board_game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_games" title="History of games">History of games</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient">Ancient</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Board_game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Ancient">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Classical board games are divided into four categories of game: race games (such as <a href="/wiki/Pachisi" title="Pachisi">Pachisi</a>), space games (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noughts_and_Crosses" title="Noughts and Crosses">Noughts and Crosses</a>), chase games (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hnefatafl" title="Hnefatafl">Hnefatafl</a>), and games of displacement (such as <a href="/wiki/Chess" title="Chess">chess</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Board games have been played, traveled, and evolved<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> in most cultures and societies throughout history. A number of important historical sites, artifacts and documents shed light on early board games such as <a href="/wiki/Jiroft_culture" title="Jiroft culture">Jiroft civilization</a> gameboards<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> in Iran. <a href="/wiki/Senet" title="Senet">Senet</a>, found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predynastic_Egypt" title="Predynastic Egypt">Predynastic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_dynasty_of_Egypt" title="First dynasty of Egypt">First Dynasty</a> burials of Egypt, c. 3500 BC and 3100 BC respectively,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-senet1_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-senet1-5">[5]</a></sup> is the oldest board game known to have existed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Senet was pictured in a <a href="/wiki/Fresco" title="Fresco">fresco</a> found in <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Merknera&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Merknera (page does not exist)">Merknera</a>'s tomb (3300–2700 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-senet2_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-senet2-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pivotto_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pivotto-8">[8]</a></sup> Also from predynastic Egypt is <a href="/wiki/Mehen_(game)" title="Mehen (game)">Mehen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hounds_and_jackals" title="Hounds and jackals">Hounds and jackals</a>, another ancient Egyptian board game, appeared around 2000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The first complete set of this game was discovered from a <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Egypt" title="Thebes, Egypt">Theban tomb</a> that dates to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/13th_dynasty" title="13th dynasty">13th dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> This game was also popular in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Backgammon" title="Backgammon">Backgammon</a> originated in ancient Mesopotamia about 5,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ashtapada" title="Ashtapada">Ashtapada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chess" title="Chess">Chess</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pachisi" title="Pachisi">Pachisi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chaupar" title="Chaupar">Chaupar</a> originated in India. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Go_(board_game)" title="Go (board game)">Go</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liubo" title="Liubo">Liubo</a> originated in China. <a href="/wiki/Patolli" title="Patolli">Patolli</a> originated in <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a> played by the ancient Aztecs and The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Game_of_Ur" title="Royal Game of Ur">Royal Game of Ur</a> was found in the Royal Tombs of Ur, dating to Mesopotamia 4,600 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fv_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-fv-15">[15]</a></sup> The earliest known games list is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddha_games_list" title="Buddha games list">Buddha games list</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European">European</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Board_game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: European">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Eurogame#History" title="Eurogame">Eurogame § History</a></div>
<p>Board games have a long tradition in Europe. The oldest records of board gaming in Europe date back to <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a> (written in the 8th century BC), in which he mentions the Ancient Greek game of <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Petteia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Petteia (page does not exist)">Petteia</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-16">[16]</a></sup> This game of <i>petteia</i> would later evolve into the Roman <i>Ludus Latrunculorum</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-16">[16]</a></sup> Board gaming in ancient Europe was not unique to the Greco-Roman world, with records estimating that the ancient Norse game of <i>Hnefatafl</i> was developed sometime before 400AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> In ancient Ireland, the game of <i><a href="/wiki/Fidchell" title="Fidchell">Fidchell</a></i> or <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ficheall&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ficheall (page does not exist)">Ficheall</a></i>, is said to date back to at least 144 AD,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> though this is likely an anachronism. A fidchell board dating from the 10th century has been uncovered in Co. Westmeath, Ireland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>The association of dice and cards with gambling led to all dice games except backgammon being treated as lotteries by dice in the gaming acts of <a href="/wiki/Gaming_Act_1710" title="Gaming Act 1710">1710</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaming_Act_1845" title="Gaming Act 1845">1845</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Early board game producers in the second half of the eighteenth century were mapmakers. The global popularization of Board Games, with special themes and branding, coincided with the formation of the global dominance of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Wallis_(publisher)" title="John Wallis (publisher)">John Wallis</a> was an English board game publisher, bookseller, map/chart seller, printseller, music seller, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cartographer" title="Cartographer">cartographer</a>. With his sons John Wallis Jr. and Edward Wallis, he was one of the most prolific publishers of board games of the late 18th and early 19th centuries.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> John Betts' <i>A Tour of the British Colonies and Foreign Possessions</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> and William Spooner's <i>A Voyage of Discovery</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> were popular in the British empire. <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Kriegsspiel" title="Kriegsspiel">Kriegsspiel</a></i></span> is a genre of wargaming developed in 19th century <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a> to teach battle tactics to officers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 129.33333333333px"><div style="width: 129.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 127.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Attic_Black-Figure_Neck_Amphora_-_Achilles_and_Ajax_playing_a_board_game_overseen_by_Athena.jpg" title="Achilles and Ajax playing a board game overseen by Athena, Attic Black-figure Neck Amphora, ca. 510 BCE"><img alt="Achilles and Ajax playing a board game overseen by Athena, Attic Black-figure Neck Amphora, ca. 510 BCE" data-file-height="4190" data-file-width="3335" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Attic_Black-Figure_Neck_Amphora_-_Achilles_and_Ajax_playing_a_board_game_overseen_by_Athena.jpg/191px-Attic_Black-Figure_Neck_Amphora_-_Achilles_and_Ajax_playing_a_board_game_overseen_by_Athena.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Attic_Black-Figure_Neck_Amphora_-_Achilles_and_Ajax_playing_a_board_game_overseen_by_Athena.jpg/286px-Attic_Black-Figure_Neck_Amphora_-_Achilles_and_Ajax_playing_a_board_game_overseen_by_Athena.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Attic_Black-Figure_Neck_Amphora_-_Achilles_and_Ajax_playing_a_board_game_overseen_by_Athena.jpg/382px-Attic_Black-Figure_Neck_Amphora_-_Achilles_and_Ajax_playing_a_board_game_overseen_by_Athena.jpg 2x" width="128"/></a></div></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="American">American</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Board_game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: American">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 17th- and 18th-century colonial America, the agrarian life of the country left little time for game playing,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> although <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Draughts" title="Draughts">draughts</a> (<a href="/wiki/Checkers" title="Checkers">checkers</a>), <a href="/wiki/Bowling" title="Bowling">bowling</a>, and card games were not unknown. The Pilgrims and Puritans of <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a> frowned on game-playing, and they often viewed dice as instruments of the devil. When Governor William Bradford discovered a group of non-Puritans playing stool-ball, pitching the bar, and pursuing other sports in the streets on Christmas Day, 1622, he confiscated their implements, reprimanded them, and told them their devotion for the day should be confined to their homes.
</p><p>
In <i>Thoughts on Lotteries</i> (1826), <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> wrote: </p><blockquote><p>Almost all these pursuits of chance [i.e., of human industry] produce something useful to society. But there are some which produce nothing, and endanger the well-being of the individuals engaged in them or of others depending on them. Such are games with cards, dice, billiards, etc. And although the pursuit of them is a matter of natural right, yet society, perceiving the irresistible bent of some of its members to pursue them, and the ruin produced by them to the families depending on these individuals, consider it as a case of insanity, quoad hoc, step in to protect the family and the party himself, as in other cases of insanity, infancy, imbecility, etc., and suppress the pursuit altogether, and the natural right of following it. There are some other games of chance, useful on certain occasions, and injurious only when carried beyond their useful bounds. Such are insurances, <a href="/wiki/Lottery" title="Lottery">lotteries</a>, raffles, etc. These they do not suppress, but take their regulation under their own discretion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FirstAmericanPrintrunOfThe_MansionOfHappiness.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="992" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/FirstAmericanPrintrunOfThe_MansionOfHappiness.jpg/220px-FirstAmericanPrintrunOfThe_MansionOfHappiness.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/FirstAmericanPrintrunOfThe_MansionOfHappiness.jpg/330px-FirstAmericanPrintrunOfThe_MansionOfHappiness.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/FirstAmericanPrintrunOfThe_MansionOfHappiness.jpg/440px-FirstAmericanPrintrunOfThe_MansionOfHappiness.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FirstAmericanPrintrunOfThe_MansionOfHappiness.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Mansion of Happiness</i> (1843)</div></div></div>
<p>The board game <i>Traveller's Tour Through the United States</i> and its sister game <i>Traveller's Tour Through Europe</i> were published by New York City bookseller F. &amp; R. Lockwood in 1822 and claim the distinction of being the first board games published in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fv_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-fv-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>As the U.S. shifted from agrarian to urban living in the 19th century, greater leisure time and a rise in income became available to the middle class. The American home, once the center of economic production, became the <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/locus" title="wikt:locus">locus</a> of entertainment, enlightenment, and education under the supervision of mothers. Children were encouraged to play board games that developed literacy skills and provided moral instruction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jensen_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jensen-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest board games published in the United States were based upon Christian morality. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mansion_of_Happiness" title="The Mansion of Happiness">The Mansion of Happiness</a></i> (1843), for example, sent players along a path of virtues and vices that led to the Mansion of Happiness (Heaven).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jensen_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jensen-26">[26]</a></sup> <i>The Game of Pope and Pagan, or The Siege of the Stronghold of Satan by the Christian Army</i> (1844) pitted an image on its board of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> woman committing <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suttee" title="Suttee">suttee</a></i> against missionaries landing on a foreign shore. The missionaries are cast in white as "the symbol of innocence, temperance, and hope" while the pope and pagan are cast in black, the color of "gloom of error, and ... grief at the daily loss of empire".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Commercially produced board games in the mid-19th century were monochrome prints laboriously hand-colored by teams of low-paid young factory women. Advances in papermaking and printmaking during the period enabled the commercial production of relatively inexpensive board games. The most significant advance was the development of <a href="/wiki/Chromolithography" title="Chromolithography">chromolithography</a>, a technological achievement that made bold, richly colored images available at affordable prices. Games cost as little as US$.25 for a small boxed card game to $3.00 for more elaborate games.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:District_Messenger_Boy_Box_Cover_1886.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="282" decoding="async" height="301" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/District_Messenger_Boy_Box_Cover_1886.jpg/170px-District_Messenger_Boy_Box_Cover_1886.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/District_Messenger_Boy_Box_Cover_1886.jpg/255px-District_Messenger_Boy_Box_Cover_1886.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/42/District_Messenger_Boy_Box_Cover_1886.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:District_Messenger_Boy_Box_Cover_1886.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Game of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telegraph_boys" title="Telegraph boys">District Messenger Boy</a></i> (1886)</div></div></div>
<p>American Protestants believed a virtuous life led to success, but the belief was challenged mid-century when the country embraced materialism and capitalism. In 1860, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Checkered_Game_of_Life" title="The Checkered Game of Life">The Checkered Game of Life</a></i> rewarded players for mundane activities such as attending college, marrying, and getting rich. Daily life rather than eternal life became the focus of board games. The game was the first to focus on secular virtues rather than religious virtues,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jensen_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-Jensen-26">[26]</a></sup> and sold 40,000 copies its first year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hofer_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hofer-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Game of the District Messenger Boy, or Merit Rewarded</i>, published in 1886 by the New York City firm of <a href="/wiki/McLoughlin_Brothers" title="McLoughlin Brothers">McLoughlin Brothers</a>, was one of the first board games based on materialism and capitalism published in the United States. The game is a typical <a href="/wiki/Category:Roll-and-move_board_games" title="Category:Roll-and-move board games">roll-and-move</a> track board game. Players move their tokens along the track at the spin of the arrow toward the goal at the track's end. Some spaces on the track will advance the player while others will send him back.
</p><p>In the affluent 1880s, Americans witnessed the publication of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horatio_Alger,_Jr." title="Horatio Alger, Jr.">Algeresque</a> <a href="/wiki/Rags_to_riches" title="Rags to riches">rags to riches</a> games that permitted players to emulate the capitalist heroes of the age. One of the first such games, <i>The Game of the District Messenger Boy</i>, encouraged the idea that the lowliest messenger boy could ascend the corporate ladder to its topmost rung. Such games insinuated that the accumulation of wealth brought increased social status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jensen_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-Jensen-26">[26]</a></sup> Competitive capitalistic games culminated in 1935 with <i><a href="/wiki/Monopoly_(game)" title="Monopoly (game)">Monopoly</a></i>, the most commercially successful board game in U.S. history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>McLoughlin Brothers published similar games based on the telegraph boy theme including <i>Game of the Telegraph Boy, or Merit Rewarded</i> (1888). Greg Downey notes in his essay, "Information Networks and Urban Spaces: The Case of the Telegraph Messenger Boy", that families who could afford the deluxe version of the game in its chromolithographed, the wood-sided box would not "have sent their sons out for such a rough apprenticeship in the working world."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Margaret_Hofer&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Margaret Hofer (page does not exist)">Margaret Hofer</a> described the period of the 1880s–1920s as "The Golden Age" of board gaming in America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fv_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-fv-15">[15]</a></sup> Board game popularity was boosted, like that of many items, through <a href="/wiki/Mass_production" title="Mass production">mass production</a>, which made them cheaper and more easily available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walker2014_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walker2014-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Self-published_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on a self-published source. (February 2020)">self-published source?</span></a></i>]</sup> Although there are no detailed statistics, some scholars suggest that the 20th century saw a decline in the popularity of the hobby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walker2014_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Walker2014-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Chinese.2C_Arabic_and_Indian"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chinese,_Arabic_and_Indian">Chinese, Arabic and Indian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Board_game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Chinese, Arabic and Indian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Outside of Europe and the U.S., many traditional board games are popular. In China, Go and many variations of chess are popular. In Africa and the Middle East, <a href="/wiki/Mancala" title="Mancala">mancala</a> is a popular board game archetype with a lot of regional variations. In India, a community game called <a href="/wiki/Carrom" title="Carrom">Carrom</a> is popular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern">Modern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Board_game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Modern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Board_games_with_expansions_1944-2017.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="329" data-file-width="874" decoding="async" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Board_games_with_expansions_1944-2017.svg/310px-Board_games_with_expansions_1944-2017.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Board_games_with_expansions_1944-2017.svg/465px-Board_games_with_expansions_1944-2017.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Board_games_with_expansions_1944-2017.svg/620px-Board_games_with_expansions_1944-2017.svg.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Board_games_with_expansions_1944-2017.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The number of board games published by year (1944–2017), as listed on <a href="/wiki/BoardGameGeek" title="BoardGameGeek">BoardGameGeek</a>. Expansion sets for existing games are marked in orange.</div></div></div>
<p>The late 1990s onwards have seen substantial growth in the reach and market of board games. This has been attributed to, among other factors, the Internet, which has made it easier for people to find out about games and to find opponents to play against,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walker2014_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-Walker2014-31">[31]</a></sup> as well as with a general increase in leisure time and consumer spending on entertainment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Around the year 2000 the board gaming industry began significant growth with companies producing a rising number of new games to be sold to a growing worldwide audience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-board_game_resurgence_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-board_game_resurgence-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> In the 2010s, a number of publications referred to board games as having a new Golden Age, though some board-gamers prefer to call it a 'renaissance', as The Golden Age is both predefined and a common term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-board_game_resurgence_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-board_game_resurgence-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian2014oct_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardian2014oct-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Board game venues are also growing in popularity; in 2016, over 5,000 board game cafes opened in the U.S. alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Board game cafes are also reported to be very popular in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Board games have also been used as a mechanism for <a href="/wiki/Science_communication" title="Science communication">science communication</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Luck.2C_strategy.2C_and_diplomacy"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Luck,_strategy,_and_diplomacy">Luck, strategy, and diplomacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Board_game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Luck, strategy, and diplomacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Some games, such as chess, depend completely on player skill, while many children's games such as <i><a href="/wiki/Candy_Land" title="Candy Land">Candy Land</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Snakes_and_Ladders" title="Snakes and Ladders">Snakes and Ladders</a></i> require no decisions by the players and are decided purely by luck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Board_game_damah_at_Souq_Waqif.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1094" data-file-width="1647" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Board_game_damah_at_Souq_Waqif.jpg/220px-Board_game_damah_at_Souq_Waqif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Board_game_damah_at_Souq_Waqif.jpg/330px-Board_game_damah_at_Souq_Waqif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Board_game_damah_at_Souq_Waqif.jpg/440px-Board_game_damah_at_Souq_Waqif.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Board_game_damah_at_Souq_Waqif.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two Qataris playing the traditional board game of <i><a href="/wiki/Turkish_draughts" title="Turkish draughts">Damah</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Many games require some level of both skill and luck. A player may be hampered by bad luck in <a href="/wiki/Backgammon" title="Backgammon">backgammon</a>, <i>Monopoly</i>, or <i><a href="/wiki/Risk_(game)" title="Risk (game)">Risk</a></i>; but over many games a skilled player will win more often.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The elements of luck can also make for more excitement at times, and allow for more diverse and multifaceted strategies, as concepts such as <a href="/wiki/Expected_value" title="Expected value">expected value</a> and <a href="/wiki/Risk_management" title="Risk management">risk management</a> must be considered.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Luck may be introduced into a game by a number of methods. The use of <a href="/wiki/Dice" title="Dice">dice</a> of various sorts goes back to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Game_of_Ur" title="Royal Game of Ur">earliest board games</a>. These can decide everything from how many steps a player moves their token, as in <i>Monopoly</i>, to how their forces fare in battle, as in <i>Risk</i>, or which resources a player gains, as in <i><a href="/wiki/Catan" title="Catan">Catan</a></i>. Other games such as <i><a href="/wiki/Sorry!_(game)" title="Sorry! (game)">Sorry!</a></i> use a deck of special <a href="/wiki/Playing_card" title="Playing card">cards</a> that, when shuffled, create randomness. <i><a href="/wiki/Scrabble" title="Scrabble">Scrabble</a></i> does something similar with randomly picked letters. Other games use spinners, timers of random length, or other sources of randomness. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German-style_board_game" title="German-style board game">German-style board games</a> are notable for often having less luck element than many North American board games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-How_stuff_works_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-How_stuff_works-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another important aspect of some games is diplomacy, that is, players making deals with one another. Negotiation generally features only in games with three or more players, <a href="/wiki/Cooperative_board_game" title="Cooperative board game">cooperative games</a> being the exception. An important facet of <i>Catan</i>, for example, is convincing players to trade with you rather than with opponents. In <i>Risk</i>, two or more players may team up against others. <i>Easy</i> diplomacy involves convincing other players that someone else is winning and should therefore be teamed up against. <i>Advanced</i> diplomacy (e.g., in the aptly named game <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diplomacy_(board_game)" title="Diplomacy (board game)">Diplomacy</a></i>) consists of making elaborate plans together, with the possibility of betrayal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Perfect_information" title="Perfect information">perfect information</a> games, such as <a href="/wiki/Chess" title="Chess">chess</a>, each player has complete information on the state of the game, but in other games, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Tigris_and_Euphrates" title="Tigris and Euphrates">Tigris and Euphrates</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Stratego" title="Stratego">Stratego</a></i>, some information is hidden from players. This makes finding the best move more difficult and may involve estimating probabilities by the opponents.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Software">Software</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Board_game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Software">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Many board games are now available as video games. These are aptly termed digital board games, and their distinguishing characteristic compared to traditional board games is they can now be played <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">online</a> against a computer or other players. Some websites (such as boardgamearena.com, yucata.de, etc.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-onlineboard_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-onlineboard-45">[45]</a></sup> allow play in <a href="/wiki/Real_time_(media)" title="Real time (media)">real time</a> and immediately show the opponents' moves, while others use email to notify the players after each move.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chessbyemail_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-chessbyemail-46">[46]</a></sup> The Internet and cheaper home printing has also influenced board games via print-and-play games that may be purchased and printed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-printplay_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-printplay-47">[47]</a></sup> Some games use external media such as audio cassettes or DVDs in accompaniment to the game.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dvdgames_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-dvdgames-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cassettegames_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-cassettegames-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Virtual_tabletop"></span>
There are also <a href="/wiki/Digital_tabletop_game#Tabletop_simulators" title="Digital tabletop game">virtual tabletop</a> programs that allow online players to play a variety of existing and new board games through tools needed to manipulate the game board, but do not necessarily enforce the game's rules, leaving this up to the players. There are generalized programs such as <i><a href="/wiki/Vassal_Engine" title="Vassal Engine">Vassal</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Tabletop_Simulator" title="Tabletop Simulator">Tabletop Simulator</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Tabletopia" title="Tabletopia">Tabletopia</a></i> that can be used to play any board or card game, while programs like <i><a href="/wiki/Roll20" title="Roll20">Roll20</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Fantasy_Grounds" title="Fantasy Grounds">Fantasy Grounds</a></i> that are more specialized for role-playing games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Some of these virtual tabletops have worked with the license holders to allow for use of their game's assets within the program; for example, <i>Fantasy Grounds</i> has licenses for both <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Pathfinder_(periodicals)" title="Pathfinder (periodicals)">Pathfinder</a></i> materials, while <i>Tabletop Simulator</i> allows game publishers to provide <a href="/wiki/Microtransaction" title="Microtransaction">paid downloadable content</a> for their games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> However, as these games offer the ability to add in content through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mod_(video_gaming)" title="Mod (video gaming)">user modifications</a>, there are also unlicensed uses of board game assets available through these programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gamasutra_interview_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-gamasutra_interview-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Market">Market</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Board_game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Market">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nepomuk_280_-_Osadn%C3%ADci_z_Katanu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2154" data-file-width="2872" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Nepomuk_280_-_Osadn%C3%ADci_z_Katanu.jpg/220px-Nepomuk_280_-_Osadn%C3%ADci_z_Katanu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Nepomuk_280_-_Osadn%C3%ADci_z_Katanu.jpg/330px-Nepomuk_280_-_Osadn%C3%ADci_z_Katanu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Nepomuk_280_-_Osadn%C3%ADci_z_Katanu.jpg/440px-Nepomuk_280_-_Osadn%C3%ADci_z_Katanu.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nepomuk_280_-_Osadn%C3%ADci_z_Katanu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The modern German board game <i><a href="/wiki/Catan" title="Catan">Catan</a></i> is printed in 30 languages and sold 15 million by 2009.</div></div></div>
<p>While the board gaming market is estimated to be smaller than that for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Video_games" title="Video games">video games</a>, it has also experienced significant growth from the late 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian2014oct_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-guardian2014oct-36">[36]</a></sup> A 2012 article in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> described board games as "making a comeback".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Other expert sources suggest that board games never went away, and that board games have remained a popular leisure activity which has only grown over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>  Another from 2014 gave an estimate that put the growth of the board game market at "between 25% and 40% annually" since 2010, and described the current time as the "golden era for board games".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian2014oct_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-guardian2014oct-36">[36]</a></sup> The rise in board game popularity has been attributed to quality improvement (more elegant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Game_mechanic" title="Game mechanic">mechanics</a>, <dfn id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_board_games#component" title="Glossary of board games"><span class="glossary-link" style="color:inherit;" title="See entry at: Glossary of board games § component">components</span></a></dfn>, artwork, and graphics) as well as increased availability thanks to sales through the Internet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian2014oct_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-guardian2014oct-36">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Crowdsourcing" title="Crowdsourcing">Crowd-sourcing</a> for board games is a large facet of the market, with $233 million raised on Kickstarter in 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 1991 estimate for the global board game market was over $1.2 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrowneBrowne2001_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrowneBrowne2001-58">[58]</a></sup> A 2001 estimate for the United States "board games and puzzle" market gave a value of under $400 million, and for United Kingdom, of about £50 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> A 2009 estimate for the Korean market was put at 800 million won,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> and another estimate for the American board game market for the same year was at about $800 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> A 2011 estimate for the Chinese board game market was at over 10 billion <a href="/wiki/Yuan_(currency)" title="Yuan (currency)">yuan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> (Some estimates may split board games from <a href="/wiki/Collectible_card_game" title="Collectible card game">collectible card</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miniature_games" title="Miniature games">miniature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Role-playing_game" title="Role-playing game">role-playing games</a>; for example another 2014 estimate distinguishing board games from other types of hobby games gave the estimate for the U.S. and Canada market at only $75 million, with the total size of what it defined as the hobby game market at over $700 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> with a 2015 estimate suggesting a value of almost $900 million<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>) A 2013 estimate put the size of the German toy market at 2.7 billion euros (out of which, the board games and puzzle market is worth about 375 million euros), and Polish markets, at 2 billion and 280 million <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polish_zloty" title="Polish zloty">zlotys</a>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Per capita, in 2009 Germany was considered to be the best market, with the highest number of games sold per individual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Board_game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

.thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:392px;max-width:392px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:268px;max-width:268px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:176px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" style="width:120px;max-width:120px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:176px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USMC-14131.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3000" width="118"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Board games serve diverse interests. <em>Left</em>: <a href="/wiki/K%C5%8Dnane" title="Kōnane">Kōnane</a> for studious competition. <em>Right</em>: Kōnane for lighthearted fun.</div></div></div></div>
<p>A dedicated field of research into gaming exists, known as <a href="/wiki/Game_studies" title="Game studies">game studies</a> or ludology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>While there has been a fair amount of scientific research on the psychology of older board games (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Chess" title="Chess">chess</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Go_(board_game)" title="Go (board game)">Go</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mancala" title="Mancala">mancala</a>), less has been done on contemporary board games such as <i><a href="/wiki/Monopoly_(game)" title="Monopoly (game)">Monopoly</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Scrabble" title="Scrabble">Scrabble</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Risk_(game)" title="Risk (game)">Risk</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> and especially modern board games such as <i><a href="/wiki/Catan" title="Catan">Catan</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Agricola_(board_game)" title="Agricola (board game)">Agricola</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Pandemic_(board_game)" title="Pandemic (board game)">Pandemic</a></i>. Much research has been carried out on chess, in part because many tournament players are publicly ranked in national and international lists, which makes it possible to compare their levels of expertise. The works of <a href="/wiki/Adriaan_de_Groot" title="Adriaan de Groot">Adriaan de Groot</a>, William Chase, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_A._Simon" title="Herbert A. Simon">Herbert A. Simon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fernand_Gobet" title="Fernand Gobet">Fernand Gobet</a> have established that knowledge, more than the ability to anticipate moves, plays an essential role in chess-playing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Linearly arranged board games have been shown to improve children's spatial numerical understanding. This is because the game is similar to a <a href="/wiki/Number_line" title="Number line">number line</a> in that they promote a linear understanding of numbers rather than the innate logarithmic one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Research studies show that board games such as <em>Snakes and Ladders</em> result in children showing significant improvements in aspects of basic number skills such as counting, recognizing numbers, numerical estimation and number comprehension. They also practice fine motor skills each time they grasp a game piece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Playing board games has also been tied to improving children's <a href="/wiki/Executive_functions" title="Executive functions">executive functions</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> and help reducing risks of dementia for the elderly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Related to this is a growing academic interest in the topic of game accessibility, culminating in the development of guidelines for assessing the accessibility of modern tabletop games<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> and the extent to which they are playable for people with disabilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Additionally, board games can be therapeutic. <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Barrymore_Halpenny" title="Bruce Barrymore Halpenny">Bruce Halpenny</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Games_inventor" title="Games inventor">games inventor</a> said when interviewed about his game, <a href="/wiki/The_Great_Train_Robbery_(board_game)" title="The Great Train Robbery (board game)"><i>The Great Train Robbery</i></a>:</p><blockquote><p>With crime you deal with every basic human emotion and also have enough elements to combine action with melodrama. The player's imagination is fired as they plan to rob the train. Because of the gamble they take in the early stage of the game there is a build-up of tension, which is immediately released once the train is robbed. Release of tension is therapeutic and useful in our society because most jobs are boring and repetitive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TRN1976_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-TRN1976-77">[77]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Playing games has been suggested as a viable addition to traditional educational curriculum if the content is appropriate and the gameplay informs students on the curriculum content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Categories">Categories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Board_game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Categories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are a number of ways in which board games can be classified, and considerable overlap may exist, so that a game belong in several categories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fv_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-fv-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/H._J._R._Murray" title="H. J. R. Murray">H. J. R. Murray</a>'s <i>A History of Board Games Other Than Chess</i> (1952) has been called the first attempt to develop a "scheme for the classification of board games".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-80">[80]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/David_Parlett" title="David Parlett">David Parlett</a>'s <i>Oxford History of Board Games</i> (1999) defines four primary categories: <a href="/wiki/Race_game" title="Race game">race games</a> (where the goal is to be the first to move all one's pieces to the final destination), <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Space_games&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Space games (page does not exist)">space games</a> (in which the object is to arrange the pieces into some special configuration), <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Chase_games&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Chase games (page does not exist)">chase games</a> (asymmetrical games, where players start the game with different sets of pieces and objectives) and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Displace_games&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Displace games (page does not exist)">displace games</a> (where the main objective is the capture the opponents' pieces). Parlett's also distinguishes between <a href="/wiki/Abstract_strategy_game" title="Abstract strategy game">abstract</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theme_(narrative)" title="Theme (narrative)">thematic</a> games, the latter having a specific theme or frame narrative (ex. regular chess versus, for example, <a href="/wiki/Star_Wars" title="Star Wars">Star Wars</a>-themed chess).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p><br/>
The following is a list of some of the most common game categories:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 25em;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Abstract_strategy_game" title="Abstract strategy game">Abstract strategy games</a> – e.g. <a href="/wiki/Chess" title="Chess">Chess</a>, <a href="/wiki/Checkers" title="Checkers">Checkers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Go_(board_game)" title="Go (board game)">Go</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reversi" title="Reversi">Reversi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tafl_games" title="Tafl games">tafl games</a>, or modern games such as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abalone_(game)" title="Abalone (game)">Abalone</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Dameo" title="Dameo">Dameo</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Stratego" title="Stratego">Stratego</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Hive_(game)" title="Hive (game)">Hive</a></i>, or <i><a href="/wiki/GIPF_(game)" title="GIPF (game)">GIPF</a></i></li>
<li>Alignment games – e.g. <a href="/wiki/Renju" title="Renju">Renju</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gomoku" title="Gomoku">Gomoku</a>, <a href="/wiki/Connect6" title="Connect6">Connect6</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nine_men%27s_morris" title="Nine men's morris">Nine men's morris</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Tic-tac-toe" title="Tic-tac-toe">Tic-tac-toe</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Category:Auction_board_games" title="Category:Auction board games">Auction games</a> – e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Hoity_Toity" title="Hoity Toity">Hoity Toity</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Power_Grid" title="Power Grid">Power Grid</a></i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chess_variant" title="Chess variant">Chess variants</a> – traditional variants e.g. <a href="/wiki/Shogi" title="Shogi">shogi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xiangqi" title="Xiangqi">xiangqi</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Janggi" title="Janggi">janggi</a>; modern variants e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chess960" title="Chess960">Chess960</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grand_Chess" title="Grand Chess">Grand Chess</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hexagonal_chess" title="Hexagonal chess">Hexagonal chess</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alice_Chess" title="Alice Chess">Alice Chess</a></li>
<li>Configuration games – e.g. <a href="/wiki/Lines_of_Action" title="Lines of Action">Lines of Action</a>, Hexade, or <i><a href="/wiki/Entropy_(1977_board_game)" title="Entropy (1977 board game)">Entropy</a></i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Connection_game" title="Connection game">Connection games</a> – e.g. <a href="/wiki/TwixT" title="TwixT">TwixT</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hex_(board_game)" title="Hex (board game)">Hex</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Havannah" title="Havannah">Havannah</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cooperative_board_game" title="Cooperative board game">Cooperative games</a> – e.g. <i>Max the Cat</i>, <i>Caves and Claws</i>, or <i><a href="/wiki/Pandemic_(board_game)" title="Pandemic (board game)">Pandemic</a></i></li>
<li>Count and capture games – e.g. <a href="/wiki/Mancala" title="Mancala">mancala</a> games</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cross_and_circle_game" title="Cross and circle game">Cross and circle games</a> – e.g. <a href="/wiki/Yut" title="Yut">Yut</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ludo_(board_game)" title="Ludo (board game)">Ludo</a>, or <i><a href="/wiki/Aggravation_(board_game)" title="Aggravation (board game)">Aggravation</a></i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Category:Deduction_board_games" title="Category:Deduction board games">Deduction games</a> – e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Mastermind_(board_game)" title="Mastermind (board game)">Mastermind</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Black_Box_(game)" title="Black Box (game)">Black Box</a></i></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dexterity" title="Dexterity">Dexterity</a> games – e.g. <i>Tumblin' Dice</i> or <i>Pitch Car</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Category:Economic_simulation_board_games" title="Category:Economic simulation board games">Economic simulation games</a> – e.g. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Business_Game" title="The Business Game">The Business Game</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Monopoly_(game)" title="Monopoly (game)">Monopoly</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Game_of_Life" title="The Game of Life">The Game of Life</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Power_Grid" title="Power Grid">Power Grid</a></i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Educational_game" title="Educational game">Educational games</a> – e.g. <i>Arthur Saves the Planet</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Cleopatra_and_the_Society_of_Architects" title="Cleopatra and the Society of Architects">Cleopatra and the Society of Architects</a></i>, or <i>Shakespeare: The Bard Game</i></li>
<li>Elimination games – e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Draughts" title="Draughts">draughts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alquerque" title="Alquerque">Alquerque</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fanorona" title="Fanorona">Fanorona</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yot%C3%A9" title="Yoté">Yoté</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Surakarta_(game)" title="Surakarta (game)">Surakarta</a></li>
<li>Family games – e.g. <i>Roll Through the Ages</i>, <i>Birds on a Wire</i>, or <i>For Sale</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Category:Fantasy_board_games" title="Category:Fantasy board games">Fantasy games</a> – e.g. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shadows_Over_Camelot" title="Shadows Over Camelot">Shadows Over Camelot</a></i></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German-style_board_game" title="German-style board game">German-style board games</a> or <i>Eurogames</i> – e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Catan" title="Catan">Catan</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Carcassonne_(board_game)" title="Carcassonne (board game)">Carcassonne</a></i>, Decatur • The Game, Carson City, or <i><a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_(board_game)" title="Puerto Rico (board game)">Puerto Rico</a></i></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guessing_game" title="Guessing game">Guessing games</a> – e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Pictionary" title="Pictionary">Pictionary</a></i> or <a href="/wiki/Battleship_(game)" title="Battleship (game)">Battleship</a></li>
<li>Hidden-movement games – e.g. <i>Clue</i> or Escape from the Aliens in Outer Space</li>
<li>Hidden-role games – e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Mafia_(party_game)" title="Mafia (party game)">Mafia</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/The_Resistance_(game)" title="The Resistance (game)">The Resistance</a></i></li>
<li>Historical simulation games – e.g. <i>Through the Ages</i> or <i>Railways of the World</i></li>
<li>Horror games – e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Arkham_Horror" title="Arkham Horror">Arkham Horror</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></li>
<li>Large multiplayer games – e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Take_It_Easy_(game)" title="Take It Easy (game)">Take It Easy</a></i> or <i>Swat</i> (2010)</li>
<li>Learning/communication non-competitive games – e.g. <a href="/wiki/The_Ungame" title="The Ungame">The Ungame</a> (1972)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_mancala_games" title="List of mancala games">Mancala games</a> – e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wari_(game)" title="Wari (game)">Wari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oware" title="Oware">Oware</a>, or The Glass Bead Game</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multiplayer_game" title="Multiplayer game">Multiplayer games</a> – e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Risk_(game)" title="Risk (game)">Risk</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Monopoly_(game)" title="Monopoly (game)">Monopoly</a></i>, or <a href="/wiki/Four-player_chess" title="Four-player chess">Four-player chess</a></li>
<li>Musical games – e.g. <i>Spontuneous</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Category:Negotiation_tabletop_games" title="Category:Negotiation tabletop games">Negotiation games</a> – e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Diplomacy_(game)" title="Diplomacy (game)">Diplomacy</a></i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Paper-and-pencil_game" title="Paper-and-pencil game">Paper-and-pencil games</a> – e.g. <a href="/wiki/Tic-tac-toe" title="Tic-tac-toe">Tic-tac-toe</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dots_and_Boxes" title="Dots and Boxes">Dots and Boxes</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Category:Board_games_of_physical_skill" title="Category:Board games of physical skill">Physical skill games</a> – e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Camp_Granada" title="Camp Granada">Camp Granada</a></i></li>
<li>Position games (no captures; win by leaving the opponent unable to move) – e.g. <a href="/wiki/K%C5%8Dnane" title="Kōnane">Kōnane</a>, <a href="/wiki/M%C5%AB_t%C5%8Drere" title="Mū tōrere">mū tōrere</a>, or the <a href="/wiki/L_game" title="L game">L game</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Race_game" title="Race game">Race games</a> – e.g. <a href="/wiki/Pachisi" title="Pachisi">Pachisi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Backgammon" title="Backgammon">backgammon</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Snakes_and_Ladders" title="Snakes and Ladders">Snakes and Ladders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hyena_chase" title="Hyena chase">Hyena chase</a>, or <i>Worm Up</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tabletop_role-playing_game" title="Tabletop role-playing game">Role-playing games</a> – e.g. <a href="/wiki/Dungeons_%26_Dragons" title="Dungeons &amp; Dragons">Dungeons &amp; Dragons</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Category:Roll-and-move_board_games" title="Category:Roll-and-move board games">Roll-and-move</a> games – e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Monopoly_(game)" title="Monopoly (game)">Monopoly</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/The_Game_of_Life" title="The Game of Life">Life</a></i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Running-fight_game" title="Running-fight game">Running-fight games</a> – e.g. <a href="/wiki/Bul_(game)" title="Bul (game)">Bul</a></li>
<li>Share-buying games (games in which players buy stakes in each other's positions) – typically longer economic-management games, e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Acquire" title="Acquire">Acquire</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Panamax" title="Panamax">Panamax</a></i></li>
<li>Single-player <a href="/wiki/Puzzle" title="Puzzle">puzzle</a> games – e.g. <a href="/wiki/Peg_solitaire" title="Peg solitaire">peg solitaire</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sudoku" title="Sudoku">Sudoku</a></li>
<li>Spiritual development games (games with no winners or losers) – e.g. <i>Transformation Game</i> or <i>Psyche's Key</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_abstract_strategy_games#Stacking_games" title="List of abstract strategy games">Stacking games</a> – e.g. <a href="/wiki/Lasca" title="Lasca">Lasca</a> or <i><a href="/wiki/DVONN" title="DVONN">DVONN</a></i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_storytelling_games" title="List of storytelling games">Storytelling games</a> – e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Dixit_(card_game)" title="Dixit (card game)">Dixit</a></i> or <i>Tales of the Arabian Nights</i></li>
<li>Territory games – e.g. <a href="/wiki/Go_(game)" title="Go (game)">Go</a> or <a href="/wiki/Reversi" title="Reversi">Reversi</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Category:Tile-based_board_games" title="Category:Tile-based board games">Tile-based games</a> – e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Carcassonne_(board_game)" title="Carcassonne (board game)">Carcassonne</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Scrabble" title="Scrabble">Scrabble</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Tigris_and_Euphrates" title="Tigris and Euphrates">Tigris and Euphrates</a></i>, or <i><a href="/wiki/Evo_(board_game)" title="Evo (board game)">Evo</a></i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Train_game" title="Train game">Train games</a> – e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Ticket_to_Ride_(board_game)" title="Ticket to Ride (board game)">Ticket to Ride</a></i>, <i>Steam</i>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/18xx" title="18xx">18xx</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Trivia" title="Trivia">Trivia</a> games – e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Trivial_Pursuit" title="Trivial Pursuit">Trivial Pursuit</a></i></li>
<li>Two-player-only themed games – e.g. <i>En Garde</i> or <i>Dos de Mayo</i></li>
<li>Unequal forces (or "hunt") games – e.g. <a href="/wiki/Fox_games" title="Fox games">Fox and Geese</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tafl_games" title="Tafl games">Tablut</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Board_wargame" title="Board wargame">Wargames</a> – ranging from <i><a href="/wiki/Risk_(game)" title="Risk (game)">Risk</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Diplomacy_(game)" title="Diplomacy (game)">Diplomacy</a></i>, or <i><a href="/wiki/Axis_%26_Allies" title="Axis &amp; Allies">Axis &amp; Allies</a></i>, to <i><a href="/wiki/Attack!_(board_game)" title="Attack! (board game)">Attack!</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Conquest_of_the_Empire" title="Conquest of the Empire">Conquest of the Empire</a></i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Word_game" title="Word game">Word games</a> – e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Scrabble" title="Scrabble">Scrabble</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Boggle" title="Boggle">Boggle</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Anagrams_(game)" title="Anagrams (game)">Anagrams</a>, or <i>What's My Word?</i> (2010)</li></ul>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Glossary">Glossary <span class="anchor" id="Common_terms"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Board_game&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Glossary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_board_games" title="Glossary of board games">Glossary of board games</a></div>
<p>Although many board games have a <a href="/wiki/Jargon" title="Jargon">jargon</a> all their own, there is a generalized <a href="/wiki/Terminology" title="Terminology">terminology</a> to describe concepts applicable to basic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Game_mechanic" title="Game mechanic">game mechanics</a> and attributes common to nearly all board games.
</p>
